align="right">1,094,564\n</td>\n<td align="right">100.00\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><small><sup>1</sup>Population, persons aged 15 and older.</small>\n</p>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ruhnu_puukirik.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Ruhnu_puukirik.jpg/220px-Ruhnu_puukirik.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Ruhnu_puukirik.jpg/330px-Ruhnu_puukirik.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Ruhnu_puukirik.jpg/440px-Ruhnu_puukirik.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2152" data-file-height="2869" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ruhnu_puukirik.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ruhnu" title="Ruhnu">Ruhnu</a> stave church, built in 1644, is the oldest surviving wooden building in Estonia</div></div></div>\n<p>Estonia has a rich and diverse religious history, but in recent years it has become increasingly secular, with either a <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Majority" title="Majority">majority</a> of the population declaring themselves nonreligious in recent censuses, followed by those who identify as religiously "undeclared". The largest minority groups are the various Christian denominations, principally Lutheran and Orthodox Christians, with very small numbers of adherents in non-Christian faiths such as Islam or Buddhism. Other polls suggest the country is broadly split between Christians and the non-religious / religiously undeclared.\n</p><p>In ancient Estonia, prior to Christianization and according to <a href="/wiki/Livonian_Chronicle_of_Henry" title="Livonian Chronicle of Henry">Livonian Chronicle of Henry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tharapita" title="Tharapita">Tharapita</a> was the predominant deity for the <a href="/wiki/Oeselians" title="Oeselians">Oeselians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Estonia was Christianised by the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a> in the 13th century. The <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> led to the establishment of the Lutheran church in 1686. Before the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, Estonia was approximately 80% <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a>, overwhelmingly <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ivkovi\xc4\x87Haberfeld2015_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ivkovi\xc4\x87Haberfeld2015-287">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MarshallCavendish2010_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarshallCavendish2010-289">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> followed by <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> and other Protestant branches. Many Estonians profess not to be particularly religious, because religion through the 19th century was associated with German feudal rule.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> There has historically been a small but noticeable minority of <a href="/wiki/Old_Believers" title="Old Believers">Russian Old-believers</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Peipus" title="Lake Peipus">Lake Peipus</a> area in <a href="/wiki/Tartu_County" title="Tartu County">Tartu County</a>.\n</p><p>Today, Estonia\'s constitution guarantees freedom of religion, separation of church and state, and individual rights to privacy of belief and religion.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> According to the Dentsu Communication Institute Inc, Estonia is one of the <a href="/wiki/Irreligion_by_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Irreligion by country">least religious</a> countries in the world, with 75.7% of the population claiming to be <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">irreligious</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Eurobarometer" title="Eurobarometer">Eurobarometer</a> Poll 2005 found that only 16% of Estonians profess a belief in a god, the lowest belief of all countries studied.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> According to the Lutheran World Federation, the historic Lutheran denomination has a large presence with 180,000 registered members.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>New polls about religiosity in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> in 2012 by <a href="/wiki/Eurobarometer" title="Eurobarometer">Eurobarometer</a> found that Christianity is the largest religion in Estonia accounting for 45% of <a href="/wiki/Estonians" title="Estonians">Estonians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EUROBAROMETER_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUROBAROMETER-294">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> are the largest Christian group in Estonia, accounting for 17% of Estonia citizens,<sup id="cite_ref-EUROBAROMETER_294-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUROBAROMETER-294">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestants</a> make up 6%, and Other Christian make up 22%. <a href="/wiki/Agnostic" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnostic">Non believer/Agnostic</a> account 22%, <a href="/wiki/Atheist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheist">Atheist</a> accounts for 15%, and undeclared accounts for 15%.<sup id="cite_ref-EUROBAROMETER_294-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUROBAROMETER-294">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most recent <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a>, found that in 2015, 51% of the population of Estonia declared itself Christians, 45% <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">religiously unaffiliated</a>\xe2\x80\x94a category which includes <a href="/wiki/Atheists" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheists">atheists</a>, agnostics and those who describe their religion as "<a href="/wiki/Nothing_in_Particular" class="mw-redirect" title="Nothing in Particular">Nothing in Particular</a>", while 2% belonged to other faiths.<sup id="cite_ref-Religious_Belief_in_Central_and_Eastern_Europe_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Religious_Belief_in_Central_and_Eastern_Europe-295">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> The Christians divided between 25% Eastern Orthodox, 20% Lutherans, 5% other Christians and 1% Roman Catholic.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> While the <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">religiously unaffiliated</a> divided between 9% as atheists, 1% as agnostics and 35% as <a href="/wiki/Nothing_in_Particular" class="mw-redirect" title="Nothing in Particular">Nothing in Particular</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Korghoone.PNG" class="image"><img alt="The spire of St. Olaf&#39;s Church in Tallinn looks over the city and the Gulf of Finland" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Korghoone.PNG/220px-Korghoone.PNG" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Korghoone.PNG/330px-Korghoone.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Korghoone.PNG/440px-Korghoone.PNG 2x" data-file-width="580" data-file-height="432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Korghoone.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St._Olaf%27s_Church,_Tallinn" title="St. Olaf&#39;s Church, Tallinn">St. Olaf\'s Church, Tallinn</a> was possibly the tallest building in the world from 1549 to 1625</div></div></div> \n<p>The largest religious denomination in the country is <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a>, adhered to by 160,000 Estonians (or 13% of the population), principally ethnic Estonians. Other organisations, such as the World Council of Churches, report that there are as many as 265,700 Estonian Lutherans.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, there are between 8,000\xe2\x80\x939,000 members abroad.\n</p><p>Another major group, inhabitants who follow <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> Christianity, practised chiefly by the Russian minority, and the Russian Orthodox Church is the second largest denomination with 150,000 members. The Estonian Apostolic Orthodox Church, under the Greek-Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_Patriarchate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical Patriarchate">Ecumenical Patriarchate</a>, claims another 20,000 members. Thus, the number of adherents of Lutheranism and Orthodoxy, without regard to citizenship or ethnicity, is roughly equal. Catholics have their Latin <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Administration_of_Estonia" title="Apostolic Administration of Estonia">Apostolic Administration of Estonia</a>.\n</p><p>According to the census of 2000 (data in table to the right), there were about 1,000 adherents of the <a href="/wiki/Taaraism" class="mw-redirect" title="Taaraism">Taara faith</a><sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Maausk" class="mw-redirect" title="Maausk">Maausk</a> in Estonia (see <a href="/wiki/Maavalla_Koda" title="Maavalla Koda">Maavalla Koda</a>). The Jewish community has an estimated population of about 1,900 (see <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Estonia" title="History of the Jews in Estonia">History of the Jews in Estonia</a>). Around 68,000 people consider themselves <a href="/wiki/Atheists" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheists">atheists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pub.stat.ee_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pub.stat.ee-302">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Estonia" title="Languages of Estonia">Languages of Estonia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lenguas_fino-b%C3%A1lticas.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lenguas_fino-b%C3%A1lticas.png/200px-Lenguas_fino-b%C3%A1lticas.png" width="200" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lenguas_fino-b%C3%A1lticas.png/300px-Lenguas_fino-b%C3%A1lticas.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lenguas_fino-b%C3%A1lticas.png/400px-Lenguas_fino-b%C3%A1lticas.png 2x" data-file-width="459" data-file-height="463" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lenguas_fino-b%C3%A1lticas.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic</a> languages</div></div></div>\n<p>The official language, <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a>, belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">Finnic</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic languages</a>. Estonian is closely related to Finnish, spoken in Finland, across the other side of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Finland" title="Gulf of Finland">Gulf of Finland</a>, and is one of the few <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">Languages of Europe</a> that is not of an <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European language">Indo-European</a> origin. Despite some overlaps in the vocabulary due to borrowings, in terms of its origin, Estonian and Finnish are not related to their nearest geographical neighbours, <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a>, and Russian, which are all <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>.\n</p><p>Although the Estonian and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic language">Germanic languages</a> are of very different origins, one can identify many similar words in Estonian and German, for example. This is primarily because the Estonian language has borrowed nearly one third of its vocabulary from <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>, mainly from Low Saxon (<a href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German" title="Middle Low German">Middle Low German</a>) during the period of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Estonia#Estonian_Crusade:_The_Middle_Ages" title="History of Estonia">German rule</a>, and <a href="/wiki/High_German" class="mw-redirect" title="High German">High German</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Standard_German" title="Standard German">standard German</a>). The percentage of Low Saxon and High German loanwords can be estimated at 22\xe2\x80\x9325 percent, with Low Saxon making up about 15 percent.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/South_Estonian" title="South Estonian">South Estonian</a> languages are spoken by 100,000 people and include the dialects of <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B5ro_language" title="V\xc3\xb5ro language">V\xc3\xb5ro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seto_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Seto language">Seto</a>. The languages are spoken in South-Eastern Estonia, are <a href="/wiki/Genetic_relationship_(linguistics)" title="Genetic relationship (linguistics)">genealogically</a> distinct from northern Estonian: but are traditionally and officially considered as <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a> and "regional forms of the Estonian language", not separate language(s).<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Russian is still spoken as a secondary language by forty- to seventy-year-old ethnic Estonians, because Russian was the unofficial language of the <a href="/wiki/Estonian_SSR" class="mw-redirect" title="Estonian SSR">Estonian SSR</a> from 1944 to 1991 and taught as a compulsory second language during the Soviet era. In 1998, most first- and second-generation industrial immigrants from the former Soviet Union (mainly the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">Russian SFSR</a>) did not speak Estonian.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> However, by 2010, 64.1% of non-ethnic Estonians spoke Estonian.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> The latter, mostly Russian-speaking ethnic minorities, reside predominantly in the capital city of Tallinn and the industrial urban areas in <a href="/wiki/Ida-Viru_County" title="Ida-Viru County">Ida-Virumaa</a>.\n</p><p>From the 13th to the 20th century, there were Swedish-speaking communities in Estonia, particularly in the coastal areas and on the islands (e.g., Hiiumaa, Vormsi, Ruhnu; in Swedish, known as Dag\xc3\xb6, Orms\xc3\xb6, Run\xc3\xb6, respectively) along the Baltic sea, communities which today have almost disappeared. The Swedish-speaking minority was represented in parliament, and entitled to use their native language in parliamentary debates.\n</p><p>From 1918 to 1940, when Estonia was independent, the small Swedish community was well treated. Municipalities with a Swedish majority, mainly found along the coast, used Swedish as the administrative language and Swedish-Estonian culture saw an upswing. However, most Swedish-speaking people fled to Sweden before the end of World War II, that is, before the invasion of Estonia by the Soviet army in 1944. Only a handful of older speakers remain. \nApart from many other areas the influence of Swedish is especially distinct in the <a href="/wiki/Noarootsi_Parish" title="Noarootsi Parish">Noarootsi Parish</a> of <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A4%C3%A4ne_County" title="L\xc3\xa4\xc3\xa4ne County">L\xc3\xa4\xc3\xa4ne County</a> where there are many villages with bilingual Estonian and/or Swedish names and street signs.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most common foreign languages learned by Estonian students are English, Russian, German and French. Other popular languages include Finnish, Spanish and Swedish.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_science">Education and science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Education and science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tartu_%C3%9Clikooli_peahoone_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="gray stucco building three-story building with grey slate hip roof, central portico and pediment" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Tartu_%C3%9Clikooli_peahoone_2012.jpg/220px-Tartu_%C3%9Clikooli_peahoone_2012.jpg" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Tartu_%C3%9Clikooli_peahoone_2012.jpg/330px-Tartu_%C3%9Clikooli_peahoone_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Tartu_%C3%9Clikooli_peahoone_2012.jpg/440px-Tartu_%C3%9Clikooli_peahoone_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5284" data-file-height="2792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tartu_%C3%9Clikooli_peahoone_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tartu" title="University of Tartu">University of Tartu</a> is one of the oldest universities in Northern Europe and the highest-ranked university in Estonia.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Estonia" title="Education in Estonia">Education in Estonia</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Estonia" title="List of universities in Estonia">List of universities in Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_science_in_Estonia" title="Space science in Estonia">Space science in Estonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tiigrih%C3%BCpe" title="Tiigrih\xc3\xbcpe">Tiigrih\xc3\xbcpe</a></div>\n<p>The history of formal education in Estonia dates back to the 13th and 14th centuries when the first <a href="/wiki/Monastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monastic">monastic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral schools">cathedral schools</a> were founded.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> The first primer in the Estonian language was published in 1575. The oldest university is the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tartu" title="University of Tartu">University of Tartu</a>, established by the Swedish king <a href="/wiki/Gustavus_Adolphus_of_Sweden" title="Gustavus Adolphus of Sweden">Gustav II Adolf</a> in 1632. In 1919, university courses were first taught in the Estonian language.\n</p><p>Today\'s education in Estonia is divided into general, vocational, and hobby. The education system is based on four levels: pre-school, basic, secondary, and higher education.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> A wide network of schools and supporting educational institutions have been established. The Estonian education system consists of state, municipal, public, and private institutions. There are currently 589 schools in Estonia.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_International_Student_Assessment" title="Programme for International Student Assessment">Programme for International Student Assessment</a>, the performance levels of gymnasium-age pupils in Estonia is among the highest in the world: in 2010, the country was ranked 13th for the quality of its education system, well above the OECD average.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, around 89% of Estonian adults aged 25\xe2\x80\x9364 have earned the equivalent of a high-school degree, one of the highest rates in the industrialised world.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Building_of_Estonian_Students%27_Society.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Building of Estonian Students&#39; Society in Tartu. In August 2008 a Georgian flag was hoisted besides Estonian to support Georgia in the South Ossetia war." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Building_of_Estonian_Students%27_Society.jpg/220px-Building_of_Estonian_Students%27_Society.jpg" width="220" height="103" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Building_of_Estonian_Students%27_Society.jpg/330px-Building_of_Estonian_Students%27_Society.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Building_of_Estonian_Students%27_Society.jpg/440px-Building_of_Estonian_Students%27_Society.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2376" data-file-height="1107" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Building_of_Estonian_Students%27_Society.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Building of the <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Students%27_Society" title="Estonian Students&#39; Society">Estonian Students\' Society</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tartu" title="Tartu">Tartu</a>. It is considered to be the first example of Estonian national architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tartu_(Russian%E2%80%93Finnish)" title="Treaty of Tartu (Russian\xe2\x80\x93Finnish)">Treaty of Tartu</a> between Finland and Soviet Russia was signed in the building in 1920.</div></div></div>\n<p>Academic higher education in Estonia is divided into three levels: bachelor\'s, master\'s, and doctoral studies. In some specialties (basic medical studies, veterinary, pharmacy, dentistry, architect-engineer, and a classroom teacher programme) the bachelor\'s and master\'s levels are integrated into one unit.<sup id="cite_ref-EurydiceSummary_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EurydiceSummary-315">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> Estonian public universities have significantly more autonomy than applied higher education institutions. In addition to organising the academic life of the university, universities can create new curricula, establish admission terms and conditions, approve the budget, approve the development plan, elect the rector, and make restricted decisions in matters concerning assets.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> Estonia has a moderate number of public and private universities. The largest public universities are the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tartu" title="University of Tartu">University of Tartu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tallinn_University_of_Technology" title="Tallinn University of Technology">Tallinn University of Technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tallinn_University" title="Tallinn University">Tallinn University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonian_University_of_Life_Sciences" title="Estonian University of Life Sciences">Estonian University of Life Sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Academy_of_Arts" title="Estonian Academy of Arts">Estonian Academy of Arts</a>; the largest private university is <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Business_School" title="Estonian Business School">Estonian Business School</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ESTCube_orbiidil_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="ESTCube-1 micro satellite orbiting globe and beaming light to Estonia" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ESTCube_orbiidil_2.jpg/220px-ESTCube_orbiidil_2.jpg" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ESTCube_orbiidil_2.jpg/330px-ESTCube_orbiidil_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/ESTCube_orbiidil_2.jpg/440px-ESTCube_orbiidil_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6480" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ESTCube_orbiidil_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/ESTCube-1" title="ESTCube-1">ESTCube-1</a> is the first Estonian satellite.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Estonian Academy of Sciences">Estonian Academy of Sciences</a> is the <a href="/wiki/National_academy" title="National academy">national academy</a> of science. The strongest public non-profit research institute that carries out fundamental and applied research is the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Chemical_Physics_and_Biophysics" title="National Institute of Chemical Physics and Biophysics">National Institute of Chemical Physics and Biophysics</a> (NICPB; Estonian KBFI). The first computer centres were established in the late 1950s in Tartu and Tallinn. Estonian specialists contributed in the development of software engineering standards for ministries of the Soviet Union during the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Kalja_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kalja-317">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jaakkola_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jaakkola-318">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> As of  2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Estonia spends around 2.38% of its GDP on <a href="/wiki/Research_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Research and Development">Research and Development</a>, compared to an EU average of around 2.0%.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some of the best-known scientists related to Estonia include astronomers <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Georg_Wilhelm_von_Struve" title="Friedrich Georg Wilhelm von Struve">Friedrich Georg Wilhelm von Struve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_%C3%96pik" title="Ernst \xc3\x96pik">Ernst \xc3\x96pik</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jaan_Einasto" title="Jaan Einasto">Jaan Einasto</a>, biologist <a href="/wiki/Karl_Ernst_von_Baer" title="Karl Ernst von Baer">Karl Ernst von Baer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jakob_von_Uexk%C3%BCll" title="Jakob von Uexk\xc3\xbcll">Jakob von Uexk\xc3\xbcll</a>, chemists <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Ostwald" title="Wilhelm Ostwald">Wilhelm Ostwald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schmidt_(chemist)" title="Carl Schmidt (chemist)">Carl Schmidt</a>, economist <a href="/wiki/Ragnar_Nurkse" title="Ragnar Nurkse">Ragnar Nurkse</a>, mathematician <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Krahn" title="Edgar Krahn">Edgar Krahn</a>, medical researchers <a href="/wiki/Ludvig_Puusepp" title="Ludvig Puusepp">Ludvig Puusepp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikolay_Pirogov" title="Nikolay Pirogov">Nikolay Pirogov</a>, physicist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Johann_Seebeck" title="Thomas Johann Seebeck">Thomas Johann Seebeck</a>, political scientist <a href="/wiki/Rein_Taagepera" title="Rein Taagepera">Rein Taagepera</a>, psychologist <a href="/wiki/Endel_Tulving" title="Endel Tulving">Endel Tulving</a> and <a href="/wiki/Risto_N%C3%A4%C3%A4t%C3%A4nen" title="Risto N\xc3\xa4\xc3\xa4t\xc3\xa4nen">Risto N\xc3\xa4\xc3\xa4t\xc3\xa4nen</a>, semiotician <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Lotman" title="Yuri Lotman">Yuri Lotman</a>.\n</p><p>According to <i>New Scientist</i>, Estonia will be the first nation to provide personal genetic information service sponsored by the state. They aim to minimize and prevent future ailments for those whose genes make them extra prone to conditions like adult-onset diabetes and cardiovascular diseases. The government plans to provide lifestyle advice based on the DNA for 100,000 of its 1.3 million citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:KUMU_-_The_Art_Museum_of_Estonia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/KUMU_-_The_Art_Museum_of_Estonia.jpg/200px-KUMU_-_The_Art_Museum_of_Estonia.jpg" width="200" height="136" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/KUMU_-_The_Art_Museum_of_Estonia.jpg/300px-KUMU_-_The_Art_Museum_of_Estonia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/KUMU_-_The_Art_Museum_of_Estonia.jpg/400px-KUMU_-_The_Art_Museum_of_Estonia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3943" data-file-height="2688" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/KUMU_(museum)" class="mw-redirect" title="KUMU (museum)">KUMU</a> Art Museum in Tallinn</div></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Estonia" title="Culture of Estonia">Culture of Estonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Estonians" title="List of Estonians">List of Estonians</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Estonia" title="Culture of Estonia">culture of Estonia</a> incorporates indigenous heritage, as represented by the Estonian language and the <a href="/wiki/Sauna" title="Sauna">sauna</a>, with mainstream <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic</a> and European cultural aspects. Because of its history and geography, Estonia\'s culture has been influenced by the traditions of the adjacent area\'s various Finnic, Baltic, Slavic and Germanic peoples as well as the cultural developments in the former dominant powers Sweden and Russia.\n</p><p>Today, Estonian society encourages liberty and liberalism, with popular commitment to the ideals of the limited government, discouraging centralised power and corruption. The <a href="/wiki/Protestant_work_ethic" title="Protestant work ethic">Protestant work ethic</a> remains a significant cultural staple, and free education is a highly prized institution. Like the mainstream culture in the other Nordic countries, Estonian culture can be seen to build upon the ascetic environmental realities and traditional livelihoods, a heritage of comparatively widespread <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarianism</a> out of practical reasons (see: <a href="/wiki/Everyman%27s_right" class="mw-redirect" title="Everyman&#39;s right">Everyman\'s right</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a>), and the ideals of closeness to nature and <a href="/wiki/Self-sufficiency" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-sufficiency">self-sufficiency</a> (see: <a href="/wiki/Summer_cottage" class="mw-redirect" title="Summer cottage">summer cottage</a>).\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Academy_of_Arts" title="Estonian Academy of Arts">Estonian Academy of Arts</a> (Estonian: <i>Eesti Kunstiakadeemia</i>, EKA) is providing higher education in art, design, architecture, media, art history and conservation while Viljandi Culture Academy of <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tartu" title="University of Tartu">University of Tartu</a> has an approach to popularise native culture through such curricula as native construction, native blacksmithing, native textile design, traditional handicraft and traditional music, but also jazz and church music. In 2010, there were 245 museums in Estonia whose combined collections contain more than 10&#160;million objects.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Estonia" title="Music of Estonia">Music of Estonia</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Estonian_national_awakening" title="Estonian national awakening">Estonian national awakening</a>, <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Song_Festival" title="Estonian Song Festival">Estonian Song Festival</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Estonia_in_the_Eurovision_Song_Contest" title="Estonia in the Eurovision Song Contest">Estonia in the Eurovision Song Contest</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tallinna_laululava_ansambel,_1960.a.*.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Tallinna_laululava_ansambel%2C_1960.a.%2A.JPG/220px-Tallinna_laululava_ansambel%2C_1960.a.%2A.JPG" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Tallinna_laululava_ansambel%2C_1960.a.%2A.JPG/330px-Tallinna_laululava_ansambel%2C_1960.a.%2A.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Tallinna_laululava_ansambel%2C_1960.a.%2A.JPG/440px-Tallinna_laululava_ansambel%2C_1960.a.%2A.JPG 2x" data-file-width="5670" data-file-height="3330" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tallinna_laululava_ansambel,_1960.a.*.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Song_Festival" title="Estonian Song Festival">Estonian Song Festival</a> is <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Masterpieces_of_the_Oral_and_Intangible_Heritage_of_Humanity" title="Masterpieces of the Oral and Intangible Heritage of Humanity">Masterpiece of the Oral and Intangible Heritage of Humanity</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The earliest mention of Estonian singing dates back to <a href="/wiki/Saxo_Grammaticus" title="Saxo Grammaticus">Saxo Grammaticus</a> <i>Gesta Danorum</i> (ca. 1179).<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> Saxo speaks of Estonian warriors who sang at night while waiting for a battle. The older folksongs are also referred to as <i>regilaulud</i>, songs in the poetic metre <i>regiv\xc3\xa4rss</i> the tradition shared by all <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Finns" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic Finns">Baltic Finns</a>. Runic singing was widespread among Estonians until the 18th century, when rhythmic folk songs began to replace them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Traditional wind instruments derived from those used by <a href="/wiki/Shepherd" title="Shepherd">shepherds</a> were once widespread, but are now becoming again more commonly played. Other instruments, including the <a href="/wiki/Fiddle" title="Fiddle">fiddle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zither" title="Zither">zither</a>, <a href="/wiki/Concertina" title="Concertina">concertina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Accordion" title="Accordion">accordion</a> are used to play <a href="/wiki/Polka" title="Polka">polka</a> or other dance music. The <a href="/wiki/Kannel_(instrument)" title="Kannel (instrument)">kannel</a> is a native instrument that is now again becoming more popular in Estonia. A Native Music Preserving Centre was opened in 2008 in <a href="/wiki/Viljandi" title="Viljandi">Viljandi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arvo_P%C3%A4rt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Arvo P\xc3\xa4rt bearded balding man facing left" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Arvo_P%C3%A4rt.jpg/190px-Arvo_P%C3%A4rt.jpg" width="190" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Arvo_P%C3%A4rt.jpg/285px-Arvo_P%C3%A4rt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Arvo_P%C3%A4rt.jpg/380px-Arvo_P%C3%A4rt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1512" data-file-height="1888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arvo_P%C3%A4rt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arvo_P%C3%A4rt" title="Arvo P\xc3\xa4rt">Arvo P\xc3\xa4rt</a> has been the world\'s most performed living composer since 2010.</div></div></div>\n<p>The tradition of <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Song_Festival" title="Estonian Song Festival">Estonian Song Festivals</a> (<i>Laulupidu</i>) started at the height of the <a href="/wiki/Estonian_national_awakening" title="Estonian national awakening">Estonian national awakening</a> in 1869. Today, it is one of the largest amateur <a href="/wiki/Choral" class="mw-redirect" title="Choral">choral</a> events in the world. In 2004, about 100,000 people participated in the Song Festival. Since 1928, <a href="/wiki/The_Tallinn_Song_Festival_Grounds" class="mw-redirect" title="The Tallinn Song Festival Grounds">the Tallinn Song Festival Grounds</a> (<i>Lauluv\xc3\xa4ljak</i>) have hosted the event every five years in July. The last festival took place in July 2014. In addition, Youth Song Festivals are also held every four or five years, the last of them in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Professional Estonian musicians and composers such as <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Tobias" title="Rudolf Tobias">Rudolf Tobias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miina_H%C3%A4rma" title="Miina H\xc3\xa4rma">Miina H\xc3\xa4rma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mart_Saar" title="Mart Saar">Mart Saar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artur_Kapp" title="Artur Kapp">Artur Kapp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Juhan_Aavik" title="Juhan Aavik">Juhan Aavik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aleksander_Kunileid" title="Aleksander Kunileid">Aleksander Kunileid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artur_Lemba" title="Artur Lemba">Artur Lemba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heino_Eller" title="Heino Eller">Heino Eller</a> emerged in the late 19th century. At the time of this writing, the most known Estonian composers are <a href="/wiki/Arvo_P%C3%A4rt" title="Arvo P\xc3\xa4rt">Arvo P\xc3\xa4rt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Tubin" title="Eduard Tubin">Eduard Tubin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Veljo_Tormis" title="Veljo Tormis">Veljo Tormis</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 2014, Arvo P\xc3\xa4rt was the world\'s most performed living composer for the fourth year in a row.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1950s, Estonian <a href="/wiki/Baritone" title="Baritone">baritone</a> <a href="/wiki/Georg_Ots" title="Georg Ots">Georg Ots</a> rose to worldwide prominence as an opera singer.\n</p><p>In popular music, Estonian artist <a href="/wiki/Kerli_K%C3%B5iv" class="mw-redirect" title="Kerli K\xc3\xb5iv">Kerli K\xc3\xb5iv</a> has become popular in Europe, as well as gaining moderate popularity in North America. She has provided music for the 2010 <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Alice_in_Wonderland_(2010_film)" title="Alice in Wonderland (2010 film)">Alice in Wonderland</a></i> and the television series <i><a href="/wiki/Smallville" title="Smallville">Smallville</a></i> in the United States of America.\n</p><p>Estonia won the <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest" title="Eurovision Song Contest">Eurovision Song Contest</a> in 2001 with the song "<a href="/wiki/Everybody_(Eurovision_song)" class="mw-redirect" title="Everybody (Eurovision song)">Everybody</a>" performed by <a href="/wiki/Tanel_Padar" title="Tanel Padar">Tanel Padar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Benton" title="Dave Benton">Dave Benton</a>. In 2002, Estonia hosted the event. <a href="/wiki/Maarja-Liis_Ilus" title="Maarja-Liis Ilus">Maarja-Liis Ilus</a> has competed for Estonia on two occasions (1996 and 1997), while <a href="/wiki/Eda-Ines_Etti" title="Eda-Ines Etti">Eda-Ines Etti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koit_Toome" title="Koit Toome">Koit Toome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evelin_Samuel" title="Evelin Samuel">Evelin Samuel</a> owe their popularity partly to the Eurovision Song Contest. <a href="/wiki/Lenna_Kuurmaa" title="Lenna Kuurmaa">Lenna Kuurmaa</a> is a very popular singer in Europe<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, with her band <a href="/wiki/Vanilla_Ninja" title="Vanilla Ninja">Vanilla Ninja</a>. "<a href="/wiki/R%C3%A4ndajad" title="R\xc3\xa4ndajad">R\xc3\xa4ndajad</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Urban_Symphony" title="Urban Symphony">Urban Symphony</a>, was the first ever song in Estonian to chart in the UK, Belgium, and Switzerland.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Literature_of_Estonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Literature of Estonia">Literature of Estonia</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Estophile" class="mw-redirect" title="Estophile">Estophile</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:186px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jaan_Kross_(1987)_by_Guenter_Prust.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/03/Jaan_Kross_%281987%29_by_Guenter_Prust.jpg" width="184" height="250" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="184" data-file-height="250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jaan_Kross_(1987)_by_Guenter_Prust.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jaan_Kross" title="Jaan Kross">Jaan Kross</a> is the most translated Estonian writer.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Estonian_literature" title="Estonian literature">Estonian literature</a> refers to literature written in the <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian language</a> (ca. 1&#160;million speakers).<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> The domination of Estonia after the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">Northern Crusades</a>, from the 13th century to 1918 by Germany, Sweden, and Russia resulted in few early written literary works in the Estonian language. The oldest records of written Estonian date from the 13th century. <i>Originates Livoniae</i> in <a href="/wiki/Chronicle_of_Henry_of_Livonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Chronicle of Henry of Livonia">Chronicle of Henry of Livonia</a> contains Estonian place names, words and fragments of sentences. The <i><a href="/wiki/Liber_Census_Daniae" class="mw-redirect" title="Liber Census Daniae">Liber Census Daniae</a></i> (1241) contains Estonian place and family names.<sup id="cite_ref-DWE_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DWE-327">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> Many folk tales are told to this day and some have been written down and translated to make them accessible to an international readership.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The cultural stratum of Estonian was originally characterised by a largely lyrical form of folk poetry based on syllabic quantity. Apart from a few albeit remarkable exceptions, this archaic form has not been much employed in later times. One of the most outstanding achievements in this field is the national epic <a href="/wiki/Kalevipoeg" title="Kalevipoeg">Kalevipoeg</a>. At a professional level, traditional folk song reached its new heyday during the last quarter of the 20th century, primarily thanks to the work of composer <a href="/wiki/Veljo_Tormis" title="Veljo Tormis">Veljo Tormis</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Oskar_Luts" title="Oskar Luts">Oskar Luts</a> was the most prominent prose writer of the early Estonian literature, who is still widely read today, especially his lyrical school novel <i>Kevade</i> (Spring).<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anton_Hansen_Tammsaare" title="Anton Hansen Tammsaare">Anton Hansen Tammsaare</a>\'s social epic and psychological realist <a href="/wiki/Pentalogy" title="Pentalogy">pentalogy</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Truth_and_Justice" title="Truth and Justice">Truth and Justice</a></i> captured the evolution of Estonian society from a peasant community to an independent nation.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> In modern times, <a href="/wiki/Jaan_Kross" title="Jaan Kross">Jaan Kross</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jaan_Kaplinski" title="Jaan Kaplinski">Jaan Kaplinski</a> are Estonia\'s best known and most translated writers.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup> Among the most popular writers of the late 20th and early 21st centuries are <a href="/wiki/T%C3%B5nu_%C3%95nnepalu" title="T\xc3\xb5nu \xc3\x95nnepalu">T\xc3\xb5nu \xc3\x95nnepalu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrus_Kivir%C3%A4hk" title="Andrus Kivir\xc3\xa4hk">Andrus Kivir\xc3\xa4hk</a>, who uses elements of Estonian folklore and mythology, deforming them into absurd and <a href="/wiki/Grotesque" title="Grotesque">grotesque</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Estonian_films" title="List of Estonian films">List of Estonian films</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Estonian_war_films" title="List of Estonian war films">List of Estonian war films</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Estonia" title="Cinema of Estonia">cinema of Estonia</a> started in 1908 with the production of a newsreel about Swedish King <a href="/wiki/Gustav_V_of_Sweden" class="mw-redirect" title="Gustav V of Sweden">Gustav V</a>\'s visit to Tallinn.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> The first public TV broadcast in Estonia was in July 1955. Regular, live radio broadcasts began in December 1926. Deregulation in the field of electronic media has brought radical changes compared to the beginning of the 1990s. The first licenses for private TV broadcasters were issued in 1992. The first private radio station went on the air in 1990.\n</p><p>Today the media is a vibrant and competitive sector. There is a plethora of weekly newspapers and magazines, and Estonians have a choice of 9 domestic TV channels and a host of radio stations. The Constitution guarantees freedom of speech, and Estonia has been internationally recognised for its high rate of press freedom, having been ranked 3rd in the 2012 <a href="/wiki/Press_Freedom_Index" title="Press Freedom Index">Press Freedom Index</a> by <a href="/wiki/Reporters_Without_Borders" title="Reporters Without Borders">Reporters Without Borders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Estonia has two news agencies. The <a href="/wiki/Baltic_News_Service" title="Baltic News Service">Baltic News Service</a> (BNS), founded in 1990, is a private regional news agency covering Estonia, Latvia and Lithuania. The ETV24 is an agency owned by <a href="/wiki/Eesti_Rahvusringh%C3%A4%C3%A4ling" title="Eesti Rahvusringh\xc3\xa4\xc3\xa4ling">Eesti Rahvusringh\xc3\xa4\xc3\xa4ling</a> who is a publicly funded radio and television organisation created on 30 June 2007 to take over the functions of the formerly separate <a href="/wiki/Eesti_Raadio" class="mw-redirect" title="Eesti Raadio">Eesti Raadio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eesti_Televisioon" title="Eesti Televisioon">Eesti Televisioon</a> under the terms of the Estonian National Broadcasting Act.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Estonia" title="Architecture of Estonia">Architecture of Estonia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mus%C3%A9e_de_plein_air_(Tallinn)_(7644656256).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Mus%C3%A9e_de_plein_air_%28Tallinn%29_%287644656256%29.jpg/220px-Mus%C3%A9e_de_plein_air_%28Tallinn%29_%287644656256%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Mus%C3%A9e_de_plein_air_%28Tallinn%29_%287644656256%29.jpg/330px-Mus%C3%A9e_de_plein_air_%28Tallinn%29_%287644656256%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Mus%C3%A9e_de_plein_air_%28Tallinn%29_%287644656256%29.jpg/440px-Mus%C3%A9e_de_plein_air_%28Tallinn%29_%287644656256%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mus%C3%A9e_de_plein_air_(Tallinn)_(7644656256).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A traditional farmhouse built in the <a href="/wiki/Estonian_vernacular_architecture" title="Estonian vernacular architecture">Estonian vernacular style</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The architectural history of Estonia mainly reflects its contemporary development in northern Europe. Worth mentioning is especially the architectural ensemble that makes out the medieval old town of Tallinn, which is on the UNESCO World Heritage List. In addition, the country has several unique, more or less preserved <a href="/wiki/Hill_fort" class="mw-redirect" title="Hill fort">hill forts</a> dating from pre-Christian times, a large number of still intact medieval castles and churches, while the countryside is still shaped by the presence of a vast number of manor houses from earlier centuries.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holidays">Holidays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Holidays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Estonia" title="Public holidays in Estonia">Public holidays in Estonia</a></div>\n<p>The Estonian <a href="/wiki/National_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="National Day">National Day</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(Estonia)" title="Independence Day (Estonia)">Independence Day</a> celebrated on 24 February, the day the <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Estonian Declaration of Independence">Estonian Declaration of Independence</a> was issued. As of  2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, there are 12 public holidays (which come with a day off) and 12 national holidays celebrated annually.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed" style="text-align:left; font-size:90%">\n\n<tbody><tr style="font-size:100%; text-align:left">\n<th style="width: 17em"><a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Estonia" title="Public holidays in Estonia">Public holidays in Estonia</a></th>\n<th style="width: 6em">Date\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/New_Year" title="New Year">New Year\'s Day</a></td>\n<td><small>1 January</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(Estonia)" title="Independence Day (Estonia)">Independence Day</a></td>\n<td><small>24 February</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Good_Friday" title="Good Friday">Good Friday</a></td>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Moveable_feast_(observance_practice)" class="mw-redirect" title="Moveable feast (observance practice)">moveable</a></i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Easter_Sunday" class="mw-redirect" title="Easter Sunday">Easter Sunday</a></td>\n<td><i>moveable</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/May_Day#Estonia" title="May Day">Spring Day</a></td>\n<td><small>1 May</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a></td>\n<td><i>moveable</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Victory_Day" title="Victory Day">Victory Day</a></td>\n<td><small>23 June</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Midsummer_Day#Estonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Midsummer Day">Midsummer Day</a></td>\n<td><small>24 June</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Day of Restoration of Independence</td>\n<td><small>20 August</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Christmas_Eve" title="Christmas Eve">Christmas Eve</a></td>\n<td><small>24 December</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Christmas_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="Christmas Day">Christmas Day</a></td>\n<td><small>25 December</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Boxing_Day" title="Boxing Day">Boxing Day</a></td>\n<td><small>26 December</small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Estonian_cuisine" title="Estonian cuisine">Estonian cuisine</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kama_(food)" title="Kama (food)">Kama (food)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalev_(confectioner)" title="Kalev (confectioner)">Kalev (confectioner)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kohuke" class="mw-redirect" title="Kohuke">Kohuke</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Verivorst" class="mw-redirect" title="Verivorst">Verivorst</a></div>\n<p>Historically, the cuisine of Estonia has been heavily dependent on seasons and simple peasant food. Today, it includes many typical international foods.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The most typical foods in Estonia are black bread, pork, potatoes, and dairy products.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> Traditionally in summer and spring, Estonians like to eat everything fresh \xe2\x80\x93 berries, herbs, vegetables, and everything else that comes straight from the garden. Hunting and fishing have also been very common, although currently hunting and fishing are enjoyed mostly as hobbies. Today, it is also very popular to grill outside in summer.\n</p><p>Traditionally in winter, jams, preserves, and pickles are brought to the table. Gathering and conserving fruits, mushrooms, and vegetables for winter has always been popular, but today gathering and conserving is becoming less common because everything can be bought from stores. However, preparing food for winter is still very popular in the countryside.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Estonia" title="Sport in Estonia">Sport in Estonia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tartu_Maraton_2006-3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="large crowd of skiers participating in the marathon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Tartu_Maraton_2006-3.jpg/220px-Tartu_Maraton_2006-3.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Tartu_Maraton_2006-3.jpg/330px-Tartu_Maraton_2006-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Tartu_Maraton_2006-3.jpg/440px-Tartu_Maraton_2006-3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1594" data-file-height="1072" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tartu_Maraton_2006-3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tartu_Maraton" title="Tartu Maraton">Tartu Ski Marathon</a> in 2006.</div></div></div>\n<p>Sport plays an important role in Estonian culture. After declaring independence from Russia in 1918, Estonia first competed as a nation at the <a href="/wiki/1920_Summer_Olympics" title="1920 Summer Olympics">1920 Summer Olympics</a>, although the National Olympic Committee was established in 1923. Estonian athletes took part of the Olympic Games until the country was annexed by the Soviet Union in 1940. The <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">1980 Summer Olympics</a> <a href="/wiki/Sailing" title="Sailing">Sailing regatta</a> was held in the capital city <a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">Tallinn</a>. After regaining independence in 1991, Estonia has participated in all Olympics. Estonia has won most of its medals in <a href="/wiki/Track_and_field" title="Track and field">athletics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olympic_weightlifting" title="Olympic weightlifting">weightlifting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wrestling" title="Wrestling">wrestling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cross-country_skiing" title="Cross-country skiing">cross-country skiing</a>. Estonia has had very good success at the Olympic games given the country\'s small population. Estonia\'s best results were being ranked 13th in the medal table at the <a href="/wiki/1936_Summer_Olympics_medal_table" title="1936 Summer Olympics medal table">1936 Summer Olympics</a>, and 12th at the <a href="/wiki/2006_Winter_Olympics_medal_table" title="2006 Winter Olympics medal table">2006 Winter Olympics</a>.\n</p><p>The list of notable Estonian athletes include wrestlers <a href="/wiki/Kristjan_Palusalu" title="Kristjan Palusalu">Kristjan Palusalu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kotkas" title="Johannes Kotkas">Johannes Kotkas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voldemar_V%C3%A4li" title="Voldemar V\xc3\xa4li">Voldemar V\xc3\xa4li</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Georg_Lurich" title="Georg Lurich">Georg Lurich</a>, skiers <a href="/wiki/Andrus_Veerpalu" title="Andrus Veerpalu">Andrus Veerpalu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kristina_%C5%A0migun-V%C3%A4hi" title="Kristina \xc5\xa0migun-V\xc3\xa4hi">Kristina \xc5\xa0migun-V\xc3\xa4hi</a>, fencer <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Novosjolov" title="Nikolai Novosjolov">Nikolai Novosjolov</a>, decathlete <a href="/wiki/Erki_Nool" title="Erki Nool">Erki Nool</a>, tennis players <a href="/wiki/Kaia_Kanepi" title="Kaia Kanepi">Kaia Kanepi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anett_Kontaveit" title="Anett Kontaveit">Anett Kontaveit</a>, cyclists <a href="/wiki/Jaan_Kirsipuu" title="Jaan Kirsipuu">Jaan Kirsipuu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erika_Salum%C3%A4e" title="Erika Salum\xc3\xa4e">Erika Salum\xc3\xa4e</a> and discus throwers <a href="/wiki/Gerd_Kanter" title="Gerd Kanter">Gerd Kanter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aleksander_Tammert" title="Aleksander Tammert">Aleksander Tammert</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kiiking" title="Kiiking">Kiiking</a>, a relatively new sport, was invented in 1996 by Ado Kosk in Estonia. Kiiking involves a modified swing in which the rider of the swing tries to go around 360&#160;degrees.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nabi,_Heiki.IMG_8732.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Wrestler Heiki Nabi at 2012 Summer Olympics" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Nabi%2C_Heiki.IMG_8732.JPG/220px-Nabi%2C_Heiki.IMG_8732.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Nabi%2C_Heiki.IMG_8732.JPG/330px-Nabi%2C_Heiki.IMG_8732.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Nabi%2C_Heiki.IMG_8732.JPG/440px-Nabi%2C_Heiki.IMG_8732.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nabi,_Heiki.IMG_8732.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wrestler <a href="/wiki/Heiki_Nabi" title="Heiki Nabi">Heiki Nabi</a> at <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">2012 Summer Olympics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wrestling_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Wrestling at the Summer Olympics">wrestling</a> is Estonia\'s <a href="/wiki/Estonia_at_the_Olympics" title="Estonia at the Olympics">most successful</a> Olympic sport.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Paul_Keres" title="Paul Keres">Paul Keres</a>, Estonian and Soviet chess grandmaster, was among the world\'s top players from the mid-1930s to the mid-1960s. He narrowly missed a chance at a World Chess Championship match on five occasions.\n</p><p>Basketball is also a notable sport in Estonia. The domestic top-tier basketball championship is called the <a href="/wiki/Korvpalli_Meistriliiga" title="Korvpalli Meistriliiga">Korvpalli Meistriliiga</a>. <a href="/wiki/BC_Kalev/Cramo" class="mw-redirect" title="BC Kalev/Cramo">BC Kalev/Cramo</a> are the most recent champions, having won the league in the <a href="/wiki/2016%E2%80%9317_KML_season" title="2016\xe2\x80\x9317 KML season">2016\xe2\x80\x9317 season</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tartu_%C3%9Clikool/Rock" class="mw-redirect" title="Tartu \xc3\x9clikool/Rock">University of Tartu</a> team has won the league a record 26 times. Estonian clubs also participate in European and regional competitions. <a href="/wiki/Estonia_national_basketball_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Estonia national basketball team">Estonia national basketball team</a> previously participated in <a href="/wiki/Basketball_at_the_1936_Summer_Olympics" title="Basketball at the 1936 Summer Olympics">1936 Summer Olympics</a>, appeared in <a href="/wiki/EuroBasket" title="EuroBasket">EuroBasket</a> four times. Estonian national team also competed at the <a href="/wiki/EuroBasket_2015" title="EuroBasket 2015">EuroBasket 2015</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kelly_Sildaru" title="Kelly Sildaru">Kelly Sildaru</a>, an Estonian freestyle skier, won the gold medal in the slopestyle event in the 2016 <a href="/wiki/Winter_X_Games" class="mw-redirect" title="Winter X Games">Winter X Games</a>. At age 13, she became the youngest gold medalist to date at a <a href="/wiki/Winter_X_Games" class="mw-redirect" title="Winter X Games">Winter X Games</a> event, and the first person to win a <a href="/wiki/Winter_X_Games" class="mw-redirect" title="Winter X Games">Winter X Games</a> medal for Estonia. She has also won the women\'s slopestyle at 2015 and 2016 <a href="/wiki/Winter_Dew_Tour" class="mw-redirect" title="Winter Dew Tour">Winter Dew Tour</a>.\n</p><p>In modern-era motorsports, <a href="/wiki/World_Rally_Championship" title="World Rally Championship">World Rally Championship</a> has seen two very successful Estonian drivers reach high results, with <a href="/wiki/Markko_M%C3%A4rtin" title="Markko M\xc3\xa4rtin">Markko M\xc3\xa4rtin</a> achieving 5 rally victories and finishing 3rd overall in the <a href="/wiki/2004_World_Rally_Championship" title="2004 World Rally Championship">2004 World Rally Championship</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ott_T%C3%A4nak" title="Ott T\xc3\xa4nak">Ott T\xc3\xa4nak</a> (active driver) winning his first WRC event at <a href="/wiki/2017_Rally_d%27Italia" class="mw-redirect" title="2017 Rally d&#39;Italia">2017 Rally d\'Italia</a>. In circuit racing, <a href="/wiki/Marko_Asmer" title="Marko Asmer">Marko Asmer</a> was the first Estonian driver to test a <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a> car in 2003 with <a href="/wiki/Williams_Grand_Prix_Engineering" title="Williams Grand Prix Engineering">Williams Grand Prix Engineering</a>, in other series <a href="/wiki/Sten_Pentus" title="Sten Pentus">Sten Pentus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Korjus" title="Kevin Korjus">Kevin Korjus</a> (active driver) have enjoyed success on a global scale.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_rankings">International rankings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Estonia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" Springer. p.&#160;131. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781493922796" title="Special:BookSources/9781493922796">9781493922796</a>. <q>Estonia is considered Protestant when classified by its historically predominant major religion (Norris and Inglehart 2011) and thus some authors (e.g., Davie 2003) claim Estonia belongs to Western (Lutheran) Europe, while others (e.g., Norris and Inglehart 2011) see Estonia as a Protestant ex-Communist society.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Measuring+Police+Integrity+Across+the+World%3A+Studies+from+Established+Democracies+and+Countries+in+Transition&amp;rft.pages=131&amp;rft.pub=Springer&amp;rft.date=2015-06-10&amp;rft.isbn=9781493922796&amp;rft.aulast=Ivkovi%C4%87&amp;rft.aufirst=Sanja+Kutnjak&amp;rft.au=Haberfeld%2C+M.R.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEstonia" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-288"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-288">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">\n<cite class="citation web">Ringvee, Ringo (16 September 2011). "Is Estonia really the least religious country in the world?". <i>The Guardian</i>. <q>For this situation there are several reasons, starting from the distant past (the close connection of the churches with the Swedish or German ruling classes) up to the Soviet-period atheist policy when the chain of religious traditions was broken in most families. In Estonia, religion has never played an important role on the political or ideological battlefield. The institutional religious life was dominated by foreigners until the early 20th century. The tendencies that prevailed in the late 1930s for closer relations between the state and Lutheran church [...] ended with the Soviet occupation in 1940.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Guardian&amp;rft.atitle=Is+Estonia+really+the+least+religious+country+in+the+world%3F&amp;rft.date=2011-09-16&amp;rft.aulast=Ringvee&amp;rft.aufirst=Ringo&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEstonia" class="Z3988"></span> <span class="cs1-visible-error error citation-comment">Missing or empty <code class="cs1-code">&#124;url=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#cite_web_url" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>); </span><span class="cs1-visible-error error citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">&#124;access-date=</code> requires <code class="cs1-code">&#124;url=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#accessdate_missing_url" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-MarshallCavendish2010-289"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-MarshallCavendish2010_289-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Triin Edovald; Michelle Felton; John Haywood; Rimvydas Juskaitis; Michael Thomas Kerrigan; Simon Lund-Lack; Nicholas Middleton; Josef Miskovsky; Ihar Piatrowicz; Lisa Pickering; Dace Praulins; John Swift; Vytautas Uselis; Ilivi Zajedova (2010). <i>World and Its Peoples: Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania, and Poland</i>. <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Cavendish" title="Marshall Cavendish">Marshall Cavendish</a>. p.&#160;1066. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780761478966" title="Special:BookSources/9780761478966">9780761478966</a>. <q>It is usually said that Estonia is a Protestant country; however, the overwhelming majority of Estonians, some 72 percent, are nonreligious. Estonia is the European Union (EU) country with the greatest percentage of people with no religious belief. This is in part, the result of Soviet actions and repression of religion. When the Soviet Union annexed Estonia in 1940, church property was confiscated, many theologians were deported to Siberia, most of the leadership of Evangelical Lutheran Church went into exile, and religious instruction was banned. Many churches were destroyed in the German occupation of Estonia, from 1941 through 1944, and in World War II (1939\xe2\x80\x931945), and religion was actively persecuted in Estonia under Soviet rule 1944 until 1989, when some measure of tolerance was introduced.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=World+and+Its+Peoples%3A+Estonia%2C+Latvia%2C+Lithuania%2C+and+Poland&amp;rft.pages=1066&amp;rft.pub=Marshall+Cavendish&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.isbn=9780761478966&amp;rft.au=Triin+Edovald&amp;rft.au=Michelle+Felton&amp;rft.au=John+Haywood&amp;rft.au=Rimvydas+Juskaitis&amp;rft.au=Michael+Thomas+Kerrigan&amp;rft.au=Simon+Lund-Lack&amp;rft.au=Nicholas+Middleton&amp;rft.au=Josef+Miskovsky&amp;rft.au=Ihar+Piatrowicz&amp;rft.au=Lisa+Pickering&amp;rft.au=Dace+Praulins&amp;rft.au=John+Swift&amp;rft.au=Vytautas+Uselis&amp;rft.au=Ilivi+Zajedova&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEstonia" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-290"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-290">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.country-studies.com/estonia/religion.html">"Estonia \xe2\x80\x93 Religion"</a>. Country Studies<span class="reference-accessdate">.
